B008JFUO0Y|4.0|The term \\"cult classic\\" gets thrown around quite a bit, mostly in association with a film that does poorly at the box office or a film that is so incredibly bad that it's considered good.  However, the proper meaning of a \\"cult classic\\" has more to do with the specific fan base, rather than the amount of money it fails to bring in to theaters.  But, just because a film is considered a commercial failure doesn't mean it meets the criteria to be deemed a future \\"cult classic.\\"  To meet the criteria, word of mouth comes into heavy play, not to mention it helps when the height of the film's popularity happens to be on home video.  In recent years, films like Fight Club, Donnie Darko, Office Space, and The Big Labowski, have all been deemed \\"cult classics.\\"  If you notice, none of these films have been released within the last ten years, meaning \\"cult classics\\" are established over time.  They aren't necessarily judged in one specific viewing, but rather over the course of several viewings.  No one really knows what the next \\"cult classic\\" will be - because it just happens over time.  Regardless, any \\"cult classic\\" list MUST include the original Sam Raimi film, The Evil Dead (1981) - which spawned two sequels, and a 2013 remake/reboot.<br /><br />Produced by Sam Raimi, Bruce Campbell, and Robert G. Tapert and directed by Fede Alvarez, Evil Dead is the story about five individuals staying in a cabin in the woods.  Mia (Jane Levy) is suffering from drug addiction and her friends take her to the cabin to help her through the withdrawal process.  However, after her brother, David (Shiloh Fernandez), arrives, years of bad blood come to the surface in regards to the death of their mother (from insanity).  Unfortunately, Mia starts having strange encounters (assuming it's the withdrawal or genetic insanity) and relies on her friends - Eric (Lou Taylor), Olivia (Jessica Lucas), and Natalie (Elizabeth Blackmore), along with her brother to help her.  But, after exploring the cabin, they discover a cryptic book (The Book of the Dead) with satanic scripture and pictures that unleashes pure evil, causing them to become possessed.<br /><br />After years of rumors, the next chapter in the Evil Dead series has finally arrived.  Unlike so many other terrible horror remakes in recent years, like Halloween, Friday the 13th, and Nightmare on Elm Street, Evil Dead stays true to its roots.  In a purely brilliant move, this new Evil Dead takes place years after the original 1981 film - meaning Ash Williams (Bruce Campbell) is not completely irrelevant, even though he doesn't appear in the film.  Evil Dead reboots the franchise, but the car is still parked in the driveway.  For those that don't remember a whole lot of the original film, the bridge was out and the teens couldn't get home - so that same rundown, beat up car is still right where it was parked all those years ago when Ash was watching his friends get torn apart from the inside out.<br /><br />For the fans of the original franchise, Evil Dead is a throwback to the 400,000 low budget \\"cult classic\\" that fans know and love.  Even with this new group of friends visiting the cabin, the premise remains the same.  Once again, someone makes the mistake of reading from The Book of the Dead (even though it says not to on every single page), unleashing all hell on the cabin and those inside it.  If you're a fan of the original, and you thought that film was gory, this new film will bring back that nervous, yet exciting sense of nostalgia - full of blood, guts, bone crunching, and every form of bodily fluid you can think of.  In fact, here's a brief list of the ways this new film pays homage to the original:<br /><br />  Michigan State sweatshirt<br />  Same outdoor swing<br />  The necklace<br />  Deck of cards<br />  The clock<br />  Blood stains on the floor<br />  Chainsaw<br />  Shotgun<br />  Shaky camera shots<br />  Trees are alive<br />  Light bulb full of blood<br />  ...and much more<br /><br/>There is not one single disappointment to be found in this film.  Evil Dead is every single thing you'll want from a horror film.  From the bad (unknown) acting and pop-out, scary moments, to the \\"so cheesy, it's funny\\" moments - you'll absolutely have the time of your life with this film.  Does this film cater to lovers of the original franchise and horror enthusiasts?  Yes, absolutely.  This is not a great film to view if you're not a fan of the horror genre, but if you're willing to give it a try - you won't be disappointed.  And, if you are disappointed by it - that's too bad, because this is as great of a horror remake/reboot as you're ever going to see.<br /><br />P.S. - don't you dare leave your theater seat early...  There's a \\"groovy\\" surprise waiting for the diehard Evil Dead fans after the credits.<br /><br />Overall Film Grade:  B+<br />FNDNreview.com/>There is not one single disappointment to be found in this film. 1594746168|4.0|I've always had an interest in art and the macabre. I remember discussing the works of Joel Peter Witkin and others before him, who use(d) corpses to create art, in an anatomy for drawing class while in college. The discussions were always somewhat of a footnote, never terribly in depth. I remember finding these men and their art interesting, but never gave it much more thought until I saw this book.<br /><br />I requested The Resurrectionist for two reasons. First, I was interested in the story of Spencer Black, and how the author presented this fictional man based on actual men of the time. There's a certain dark romanticism surrounding the medical field during the turn of the century. How far would an esteemed, educated, man go to unlock the secrets of the human body? One answer would be Dr. Gregory Black getting his two adolescent boys out of bed in the middle of the night to go dig up corpses. You may think that this is greatly exaggerated fiction. It is not. The characters are not real, but the events that transpire were very real during this time in our history. It was not uncommon to find empty graves where men and women were once buried. People have always leant their bodies to science, it is only recently that they have done so willingly.<br /><br />The second reason is, as an artist, I'm always looking for inspiration and reference material for paintings. Spencer Black is depicted as a VERY talented artist and his renderings of the anatomy of mythical creatures are second to none. The pencil drawings contained in this book are very detailed, phenomenal, pieces.<br /><br />The book itself is, conveniently, broken into two sections. The first is the fictional biography of Dr. Spencer Black. It is presented well, as if put together by compiling excerpts from his personal journal as well as his brother's. It starts with his childhood and quickly moves to adulthood, encapsulating his career and his obsessions. It is paced well, at about 80 pages there isn't much room for drawing out the story needlessly. However, I think the author could have done much more with the Dr. Black and his exploits. The foundation of the writing is an interesting enough premise that this book could easily be doubled without feeling exhaustive. It does a great job at being both fantastic and believable. It toes the line of fiction and non-fiction well. Had it not said fiction in the description, I would've thought Dr. Black was an actual person and this was a real biography. Because it's fiction, I think the author could've taken it a bit further and added a bit more detail to each section of Spencer's life.<br /><br />The Codex Extinct Animalia comprises the second half of this volume. This section is filled with anatomical renderings of mythical creatures. The renderings are presented well. There are both skeletal and muscular/connective tissue plates, each annotated. Details are provided as well as footnotes about Black's process of constructing and displaying each creation. I found the renderings very enjoyable. I think they will be great reference for any artist creating fantastic and mythical creatures in their work. This is a gold mine for animators as one of the hardest parts of animating creatures is understanding their anatomy and translating skeletal/muscular structures to their movements.<br /><br />Overall this is a very enjoyable book, particularly for those who enjoy the macabre, anatomy or both. It is both interesting and entertaining. My only complaint is the length. I hope that the author's next endeavor will be much more in depth. I look forward to it!ch room for drawing out the story needlessly. However, I think the author could have done much more with the Dr. Black and his exploits. The foundation of the writing is an interesting enough premise that this book could easily be doubled without feeling exhaustive. It does a great job at being both fantastic and believable. It toes the line of fiction and non-fiction well. Had it not said fiction in the description, I would've thought Dr. Black was an actual person and this was a real biography. B00BHMETKC|5.0|Hollywood did itself proud to award this perfectly-crafted thriller the Academy Award for 2012.  It's guaranteed to have you at seat's edge, so knotted up that you'll be constantly looking at your watch not out of boredom but to be assured that the unbearable tension can't go on forever.<br /><br />Graham Greene was a writer who distinguished between his deep, soul-searching works and his who-dunnit-style mysteries, calling the former \\"literature\\" and the latter \\"entertainments.\\"  \\"Argo\\" definitely shines as a stellar example of the latter.  Unlike the socio-theological examination of life that we get from \\"There Will Be Blood\\" or \\"No Country for Old Men\\" (or, for that matter, Shakespeare's tragedies), \\"Argo\\" pieces together an historical moment with such resourceful precision that we can only marvel at the craft of the director, which manifests a restraint, patience and attention to detail comparable to that of the Iranian children whom we witness piecing together the evidence of what we're seeing from the thousands of strips that have been confiscated from the overrun American  Embassy.<br /><br />In the script John Goodman, while helping Affleck construct the ruse that will permit him to free six Americans, calls directors stupid know-nothings, assuring Affleck that the American posing as a director has the easiest role of all.  At the end of the film, when the plane is finally off the ground, that individual playing a film director approaches the real director, Affleck, and extends his hand as a gesture of thanks and admiration.  As viewers we might do the same.  However it was done, \\"Argo\\" is at once a delicate balance and an infinitely complex mosaic of small pieces.  Yet, in the grand Hitchcock manner, nothing is wasted, nothing is gratuitous.  Every piece fits so well that for two hours we return to a moment in Iran in 1979.  If we leave the theater none the wiser, we also leave it marveling at where we have just been and what we have just seen.  Acting, spectacle, film scoring--all of these components of a movie are strangely irrelevant in \\"Argo.\\" All we're left with is the film.  There is absolutely no room for clutter.<br /><br />We knew going in that Jason's journey was sheer fantasy and that the movie within the movie (\\"Argo\\") was a sheer ruse.  Yet we need no convincing that the \\"Argo\\" we've just experienced was as real and believable as a film can be.  If Affleck's Argo played somewhat loose with the facts, it proves no less compelling than the mythological Argo--or the deception that proved successful to the mission.  In fact, the film succeeds because of the believability of 3 Argo's: the mythological story that has captivated readers for many centuries; the film fantasy concocted to contrive a release of the prisoners; the film we have just viewed and experienced for two breathless hours.<br /><br />There are three definitions of film that in my experience account for the compelling power of this art form to strike the sparks of truth from the stuff of imagination.  Orson Welles' \\"Ribbon of Dreams\\" is not nearly as flimsy and fantastical as it may initially appear.  From the scenario proposed by Affleck's character to the blocking out of characters and action, the entire project hung explicitly on cinema's hold on the imagination, its power to engage the viewer in a willing suspension of disbelief no less \\"real\\" than the most vivid dream or nightmare.  Second is Jean Luc Godard's definition, \\"film is truth 24 times a second,\\" reminding us that film in our post-Einstein quantum-mechanical world of the \\"dynamic\\" comes closer to representing a reality that is always changing, capturing our present experience more accurately than any form that restricts meaning to a \\"Bergsonian, knife-edge instant.\\"<br /><br />But it finally is Siegfried Krackauer's definition of film that brings to film its moral compass while emphasizing the awesome responsibility of the filmmaker. At its best, film has the potential, beyond any other visual form, of being a \\"redemption of physical reality.\\"  The very last words of the film, some of the first from a recognizable public figure, are those of Jimmy Carter, who validates all that we have just seen, providing that final piece of evidence (while satisfying Krackhauer's requirement) that we need to make Argo more than a myth, a story, even a movie.  It's an historical experience, one that has been saved from all the competing \\"noise\\" in politics, war and history to resonate in our imaginations long beyond the lives of any of its participants, creators, and possibly even later participants.<br /><br />Post-script: seeing \\"Argo\\" during the Easter season and in view of Krackauer's contention, I couldn't help but muse over film's redemptive quality in relation to the Incarnation and Resurrection.  Conclusion: film can preserve the miracle of the Incarnation, representing figures who appear to represent a supernatural, or spiritual, ancestry.  It cannot however work in the reverse direction, however, suspending the physical and temporal world of experience for the sake of representing the supernatural.  The same lens that verifies the fleshliness of the spiritual is powerless to demonstrate the spiritual component of the physical world.  In fact, there is no film director, no medium, no church, no creed, no book, no tradition capable of making the case for the divine in the material world.  As Tennyson puts in, \\"There lives more faith in honest doubt than in half the creeds.\\"  Proof of the resurrection, in fact, demands doubt in the evidence of the eye, the ear, the mind--with respect to the existence of the divine the material world is a dark veil of ignorance penetrable only by the individual's spiritual eye of belief.y other visual form, of being a \\"redemption of physical reality.\\"  The very last words of the film, some of the first from a recognizable public figure, are those of Jimmy Carter, who validates all that we have just seen, providing that final piece of evidence (while satisfying Krackhauer's requirement) that we need to make Argo more than a myth, a story, even a movie. It cannot however work in the reverse direction, however, suspending the physical and temporal world of experience for the sake of representing the supernatural.  The same lens that verifies the fleshliness of the spiritual is powerless to demonstrate the spiritual component of the physical world.  In fact, there is no film director, no medium, no church, no creed, no book, no tradition capable of making the case for the divine in the material world.  As Tennyson puts in, \\"There lives more faith in honest doubt than in half the creeds.\\"  Proof of the resurrection, in fact, demands doubt in the evidence of the eye, the ear, the mind--with respect to the existence of the divine the material world is a dark veil of ignorance penetrable only by the individual's spiritual eye of belief.

B009LMTDL0|5.0|I didn't know much about Sheryl Sandberg until I started hearing about her forth-coming book in the blogosphere.  The first few reviews I read accused her of being part of the wealthy elite who attained all of her success not on her merit, but because she was in the right place at the right time.  Other reviews accused her of blaming the victim.<br /><br />I just finished reading the book and before I wrote this review, I read the 1-star reviews which accuse her of primarily the same things.  Interestingly enough the majority of negative reviews are written by men.<br /><br />So, with all of that said... I loved this book.<br /><br />As a woman who climbed the corporate ladder, or 'jungle gym' as Sandberg more aptly describes it, I agree with her 100%.  I have witnessed many of the same behaviors in the workplace that she has witnessed and is now trying admirably to address.  I relate to her many stories of not fitting in, feeling undeserving and at times inadequate and guilty.  I think this topic is something that we as women (and professionally and emotionally evolved men) must embrace.<br /><br />The sad fact is that so many women aren't hearing the true message or reading it for themselves.  I read about one woman who has started a movement of 'lean out' in response to Sandberg's message and frankly, it's baffling.  As I was reading this book, I never got the sense that Sandberg was victim-blaming or encouraging lower income working women and/or single mothers to work harder, longer hours at the expense of their relationships with their kids.  I think that is the guilt speaking.  These women who already feel guilty are finding an easy target in Sandberg.<br /><br />I also never got the impression that she is an elitist.  Yes, as a prominent executive at Facebook she has powerful friends and breathes rarified air but she came across as very human and relatable.  Her problems are problems common to all women and she speaks to them with amazing candor, self-deprecation and humor.<br /><br />I own my own company now and no longer work in corporate America but, as a National Sales Trainer for a Fortune 100 company, I worked with many women in the workplace who leaned back.  I never really understood why.  I always leaned in, even as I was called every name in the book including bitch, arrogant and even ruthless by my 7th grade teacher.<br /><br />The bottom line is that Sandberg makes a passionate plea for equality for women AND men whether they want to stay at home to care for their families full-time or work outside the home.  She gives women on the jungle gym great advice for navigating their career and personal life.<br /><br />I'm happy I read the book to form my own opinion independent of the ignorant blowhards and women content to blame the 'glass ceiling' on their lack of opportunity.  As Sandberg points out \\"opportunities are rarely given; they are seized.\\"<br /><br />I'm firmly on the Lean In bandwagon.  And I'm a new fan of Sheryl Sandberg.tion and humor.<br /><br />I own my own company now and no longer work in corporate America but, as a National Sales Trainer for a Fortune 100 company, I worked with many women in the workplace who leaned back. B000063DFS|2.0|Wow.  What happened?  Suddenly Ozzy's songwriting just went down the toilet for this effort.  Even though I've never been the biggest fan of Zakk Wylde's frequently overbearing guitar style, I have actually learned to appreciate his playing on No Rest for the Wicked (and this album too, for that matter) but something went horribly wrong with No More Tears.  It's like Ozzy realized he didn't need to concern himself with top notch songwriting, great guitar riffs and vocal melodies in order to sell an album, because I'll be darned- No More Tears features *none* of the things that I've spent years admiring about Ozzy's music.<br /><br />\\"Mr. Tinkertrain\\" has disturbing sexual-related lyrics but who really cares?  It's the bland straight forward and uncreative way the verse melody and chorus is written that's the biggest reason for its unwillingness to perk my interest, and it's not like the album improves with the follow-up song either.  \\"I Don't Want To Change the World\\" has a *ridiculously* simplistic chorus.  \\"IIIIII don't want to change the world, I don't want the world to change me!\\"  Just terrible.  The verse and chorus both sound the same.  Are they?  Oh but it gets worse- \\"Desire\\" has the worst chorus I've ever heard from Ozzy (well, besides the one from \\"Crazy Babies\\" but that one can just be dismissed as a failed experiment on an otherwise terrific album- No Rest for the Wicked that is).  Never EVER rhyme \\"desire\\" with \\"fire\\".  It's too obvious.  Even then, if the chorus itself included better words I'd still have a major problem with the unforgiveable blandness of the tune.<br /><br />\\"Road to Nowhere\\" is an even bigger insult for all the ballad lovers out there- it's one of the most predictably written ones in Ozzy's career.  Whereas \\"Desire\\" is predictable, a song like \\"Zombie Stomp\\" fails for having one of the worst choruses ever.  \\"Hey hey do the zombie stomp, HEY HEY do the zombie stomp!\\"  Must be Ozzy's way of doing a dance tune.  It fails hard and fast.  I suppose \\"A.V.H\\" is decent enough.  Not sure why exactly- it's just as generic as \\"Desire\\".  Ozzy's vocal melody is more appealing to me on this track at any rate.  \\"Hellraiser\\" is too reminiscent of the generic hair metal scene of the 80's and wouldn't be out of place on a Poison or Motley Crue album.  I consider Ozzy's type of metal in a more inspiring category.  I admit I like Zakk's guitar line in the beginning and included again at the end, though.  The repetitive \\"I can see 'em\\" chorus from \\"S.I.N\\" is unacceptably uninteresting but the song is somewhat redeemed with a decent verse melody.  Still extremely generic.  \\"Time After Time\\" is a better ballad than \\"Road to Nowhere\\".  It's pretty melodic and good enough.<br /><br />For years the semi-chaotic nature of Zakk's guitar playing was a major distraction for me, but I have warmed up to it over the years.  I must be getting generous in my old age!  It was wrong for me to place blame on Zakk's guitar work as the sole reason No More Tears is such a disaster.  It's actually Ozzy's weak songwriting being the biggest factor.  Even with all this said, even recommending No More Tears just because it's coming from a talented and successful heavy metal musician is an impossible task since even in terms of sheer rocking out, the album comes up short in that regard as well.  A must avoid but please *do* pick up No Rest for the Wicked which takes Zakk Wylde's style and combines it with Ozzy's memorable songwriting/vocal melodies.  No More Tears is a rare example where the two biggest hits- \\"Mama I'm Coming Home\\" and the interesting arrangements on the title track that perfectly blends ethereal vocals underneath really heavy and creative guitar work- are the high points.

1400069807|5.0|Michael Moss puts forth his talent for investigation and clear and descriptive writing in \\"Salt, Sugar, Fat: How the Food Giants Hooked Us.\\"  Not only is this book very well organized, presented, and written - it is published at an important time.  I think in recent years more and more Americans (though still are the minority) are not only more aware of what's in the foods we eat, but they are actually doing something about it.  People are making changes in their eating choices and habits.  Another concept that makes this book's timing ideal is the recent promotions and actions of certain city (and other government bodies) regulating and/or banning certain ingredients and foods.<br /><br />Origins and the development of some food corporations are covered in \\"SSF\\" and their attempts to make and provide (sell) the most tasty product to the public.  Numerous laboratories test human taste-buds and preference testing is conducted.  And herein lies the dilemma: the public voluntarily (for the most part) chooses the food it buys and eats.  If some of the salt, sugar, and fat ratio is reduced or is not included in the the item (food) - it does not taste good, and therefore no one buys it, and the product and even company may shut down to a halt.  In addition to the consumers, the government strongly backs the food corporations and food lobbyists more than the consumer-health issue (which is true in all industries and realms of American life when corporations & their lobbyist interests conflict with the individual or public).  Even the processed and canned food labeling, which is a blatant lie and deceit by omission at best - is aided and abetted by the US government.<br /><br />As systematic and methodical as these food giants and their lobbyists are, what responsibility for the morbid American diet is because of - us - the public?  The consumers?<br /><br />Moss did ample research and applied hands-on ground work in 'SSF.'  Numerous pages cite his sources.  He visited company food-laboratories staffed by experienced scientific food creators/makers/tasters/enhancers.  Specific biological details are known about the human taste buds on the tongue and general physiological processes. In these 'food' labs human cravings and limits are understood like DNA testing in a crime lab.  Among the different individuals noted in SSF, is Ulfert Brookman.  With 47 years of scientific experience, he got fired by Kraft and later won a legal settlement.  This reminds me of the whistle-blowers who expose poor maintenance on air liners in order to prevent deaths, but they are drummed out of the industry with their careers ruined.  The story of Jeff Dunn is depicted by Moss. Dunn is a former marketing whiz at Coke who left the company and now promotes 'bags of carrots' using the same marketing methods.<br /><br />We are aware of why processed foods have increased so vastly in our eating culture.  American society went through significant and well-known transformations:  In sum, we have less time.  A large percentage of women are in the workplace now and have less time and energy for home-cooking, which was in previous generations a role primarily held by women.  The average commuting time in America has increased annually.  Working hours are longer.  Less time and less energy to shop for natural and healthy ingredients, prepare and cook a meal, and clean up afterwards.  Obviously, cooking is work.  A lot of work.<br /><br />On another related note to what I call the \\"morbid American diet\\" - yes, it is killing us - is that in addition to salt, sugar, and fat we have: high fructose corn syrup (HFCs), nitrates, aspartame, food coloring, and chemicals I still don't know how to pronounce.<br /><br />On the marketing angle, which is integral to our processed food mainstay, brand loyalty begins at or around the age of 12, and marketing is the art/science juxtaposed with the food scientists in the taste labs. Interesting, yet not surprising is how marketers placeparticular processed food items on eye-level locations of shelves, while certain items are at your feet at the bottom.<br /><br />With the current rise in diabetes, and obesity, and with heart disease still the number #1 killer in the US, I only see more ill people and more premature deaths because of the food we eat.  It's a cycle.  I am part of it.  I've known for years that much of the processed foods are loaded with sodium.  You cannot taste it, but it's there.<br /><br />My grandfather, who was a great cook, used to tell me, \\"we dig our graves with our knife and fork.\\"<br /><br />Michael Moss gives us a greatly needed book backed by facts to further this discussion and needed actions in our society.  Highly recommended.

159420411X|4.0|In the movie 'The Graduate', Dustin Hoffman's character is given career advice in the form of one word, 'plastics'. If a remake of the movie were to be done in present times, that word might just be 'statistics'. Nate Silver embodies this worldview and through a hodgepodge of disparate but interrelated vignettes he explores how statistics and probability play out in such areas as sports, economics, chess, poker, climate, politics, and terrorism. He is an admitted bayesian, a follower of the probabilistic approach based on the works of the 18th century reverend and mathematician Thomas Bayes, and a proponent of thinking like a fox as opposed to thinking like a hedgehog. While hedgehogs have one defining ideology or model of the world, foxes have multiple competing models, and using Bayesian probability, constantly update their predictions when new data comes in. Bayesian probability is based on subjective predictions, not on objective statistical distributions like those found in the frequentist school founded by Fisher, a 19th century biologist. It remains to be seen whether the bayesian approach is the 'true' way, but it certainly does seem more effective in practice. Silver spends a lot of time delving into the nuances of each of the example fields he explores, with just enough detail to offer insightful commentary without overwhelming the lay reader with advanced mathematics. In an increasingly unpredictable and complex world, Silver gives us a glimpse of the skills necessary to wrangle the messy reality which we find ourselves living in.

B005LAII3A|3.0|After twenty years, it was probably inevitable that Hollywood would remake \\"Total Recall.\\"  Sadly, the remake version of \\"Total Recall\\" isn't nearly as good as the original -- a giant chunk of the plot has been jettisoned (goodbye, Mars! Farewell, mutants!) and so has most of the humor and character development. Taken on its own merits, it's a decent enough sci-fi thriller, with some outstanding special effects and a solid (if underdeveloped) performance by Colin Farrell.<br /><br />On impulse, Douglas Quaid (Colin Farrell) decides to go to Rekall, a company that implants false memories for entertainment. But things instantly go very wrong -- a SWAT team bursts in and kills the Rekall employees, and on instinct Quaid kills all of them. When he runs home in a blind panic, his wife Lori (Kate Beckinsale) also tries to kill him, and reveals that she isn't even really his wife.<br /><br />Quaid goes on the run, with only a few cryptic messages and a mysterious safe-deposit box to guide him. But as Lori and the police chase him through the Colony (Australia) and the the United Federation of Britain, he meets a woman (Jessica Biel) who knows him from the past he can't remember -- and might help him bring about a new future.<br /><br />Anyone expecting a straight remake of \\"Total Recall\\" will be disappointed -- no Mars, no Bennie, no Kuato, no mysterious terraforming engines, and a good chunk of the plot twists have been removed. Oh, and no mutants... well, except for the famous three-breasted woman, whose third breast is never explained because we never see another mutant of any kind.<br /><br />No, this movie is grimly determined to be dark, gritty and \\"realistic,\\" depicting a  future where the world has been devastated into near-unlivability, and only Australia and a portion of Europe are still semi-normal. It's an intriguing idea, and it is handled in a fairly plausible way... well, except for the giant gravity elevator zipping through the Earth's mantle. That seems far-fetched.<br /><br />Len Wiseman is at his best with the action scenes -- big explosive battles involving magnetic cars, killer robots and bombs, as well as a lot of people shooting at each other. And the future societies are intriguingly-depicted -- either they're grimy, murky cyberpunk mazes (think \\"Blade Runner, but less pretty) or cold sterile technohaunts. In fact, the movie could really use some moments of humor or warmth, but neither ever appears.<br /><br />Also... what is with the lens flare? Barely a scene goes by without a lens flare trying to blind me! Was Wiseman possessed by the spirit of JJ Abrams?<br /><br />Colin Farrell gives a typically excellent performance as Doug Quaid/Carl Hauser -- he starts off as a nice ordinary guy, becomes a terrified, confused fugitive, and becomes stronger and more intense as he finds out about his past. But most of the other actors are playing roles that feel half-baked -- for instance, we don't know why Lori has such an obsessive hatred of her \\"husband.\\"  Beckinsale, Biel and Bryan Cranston give decent performances, but Bill Nighy is clearly just phoning it in.<br /><br />\\"Total Recall\\" is a mixed bag of pluses and minuses. Those seeking explosive sci-fi action and a good Colin Farrell performance will love it, but don't expect much fidelity to the film it remakes.r-fetched.<br /><br />Len Wiseman is at his best with the action scenes -- big explosive battles involving magnetic cars, killer robots and bombs, as well as a lot of people shooting at each other. Instead, this remake of the classic cult film is full of everything and nothing -- Louis Leterrier crams it with horrible acting, endless CGI, and an obnoxiously preachy plot that ends up being brain-bleedingly boring.<br /><br />A fisherman finds a floating coffin, which holds a dead princess and a still-living baby. He adopts the child, Perseus (Sam Worthington), and raises him as well -- until the god Hades (Ralph Fiennes) destroys Perseus' family and fishing boat. He's marched to the city of Argos, where he sees the king and queen declare war on the gods...<br /><br />... only to have Hades proclaim that if Princess Andromeda (Alexa Davalos) is not sacrificed within ten days, the kraken will destroy Argos. A mysterious woman named Io (Gemma Arterton) tells Perseus that he is actually the son of Zeus (Liam Neeson), and is the only hope of slaying the kraken and saving Argos.<br /><br />Honestly, the story of Perseus is one of the awesomest, most movieworthy stories in Greek mythology -- you've got a demigod hero, a sea monster, a flying horse, and a snake-headed creature that turns people to stone. But apparently, THAT story isn't good enough for the people in Hollywood. They even mock the original movie in one scene involving the mechanical owl Bubo.<br /><br />Instead director Leterrier crams all sorts of weird random stuff into the movie -- it's a constant onslaught of giant scorpions, CGI monsters, djinni, and witches who look like Guillermo del Toro discards. The plot feels skeletal and bloated at the same time, with horribly silly dialogue (\\"It is death who should be afraid of us!\\") and dozeworthy action scenes.<br /><br />Even worse, the screenwriters seem hell-bent on pushing their own theological philosophy on us (\\"God sucks and ruins everything, so don't worship him\\") NON-STOP. There's scene after scene of \\"We hate the gods! Gods are awful! Let's stupidly anger them even though they're incredibly powerful!\\"... only for them to undermine their own message in the last reel.<br /><br />And really, how can you go wrong when you have Liam Neeson and Ralph Fiennes in your movie, playing GODS? Well, you can cast the bland lump that is Sam Worthington as your lead, a pouting prettyboy who brings no charisma to his role, giving Neeson nothing to do except throw divine tantrums, and Fiennes nothing to do except slink around being vaguely sinister (since a god of death HAS to be evil, right? WRONG!). As for Gemma Arterton, she just sort of wanders in and out of the story, serving as a source of convenient information -- a complete waste.<br /><br />\\"Clash of the Titans\\" would be better titled \\"Flush of the Titans\\" -- a reeking lump of brainless cinema that stupefies even as it preaches. The original is flawed, but infinitely superior to this one.g their own theological philosophy on us (\\"God sucks and ruins everything, so don't worship him\\") NON-STOP. B001OQCV6A|4.0|Like everyone else in the world, I instantly went cross-eyed at the idea of Guy Ritchie making a Sherlock Holmes movie.<br /><br />Sure enough, this is not your dad's Sherlock. Or your grandpa's either. \\"Sherlock Holmes\\" is a thoroughly eccentric take on the legendary detective -- energetic, action-packed and clever, with more than a hint of steampunk and black magic. It gets kind of silly at times, but Robert Downey Jr. and Jude Law keep the story going strong.<br /><br />As the story opens, a young woman is about to be sacrificed in a black magic ritual by the evil Lord Blackwood (Mark Strong). However, Sherlock Holmes (Downey) and Watson (Law) thwart him just in time for the police to arrive. Three months later, Blackwood is executed -- but not before he tells Holmes that three more people will die, and the world will change.<br /><br />But then his tomb is broken open... from the inside. And at the same time, Irene Adler (Rachel McAdams) tries to hire Holmes to track down a man who is then found dead in Blackwood's tomb. As Holmes tries to unravel this case, he discovers the existence of a strange upper-crust magical cult, who are slowly being killed off by Blackwood's mysterious powers. Has Sherlock Holmes run up against supernatural forces he cannot explain or fight?<br /><br />This is not an adaptation of one of Sir Arthur Conan Doyle's stories -- this is more a movie that takes the IDEA of Sherlock Holmes and applies it to a big, bombastic, quirky action movie. Ritchie directs the movie with a sharp, kinetic style, and he keeps things from getting too stuffy with lots of fight scenes (including Holmes mapping out every fight in his head before throwing a punch).<br /><br /> His steampunky vision of London has a lot of dark, dripping corners, and Ritchie happily trots Sherlock and Watson down into them -- river boats, Parliament's basement, and grimy rituals down in some catacombs. The movie's biggest problem is that the action sometimes gets silly, such as the massive fight at the shipyard in which Holmes and Watson almost get run over by a ship.<br /><br />But he balances out all the action with plenty of funny moments (Holmes repeatedly paralyzing poor Gladstone) and witty dialogue (\\"No girl wants to marry a doctor who can't tell if a man's dead or not!\\"). This is definitely a movie that loves Holmes at his most eccentric.<br /><br />The main reason this works so well is because of Downey. He is probably one of the best actors of our age, and he gives Sherlock a bright-eyed, quirky, almost manic energy, as well as a touch of Asperger's (he literally CANNOT understand why Watson would want to get married and retire from crimefighting). You can truly believe that this guy has a brain like a supercomputer.<br /><br />Law also gives an excellent performance as a young, vital Watson who seems to have a love-hate relationship with Holmes -- they're best friends, but Holmes' eccentricities are driving him nuts. Rachel McAdams gives a solid performance as Holmes' lady thief ex-girlfriend, and Mark Strong is wonderfully, sonorously sinister as the villain, who may or may not be a sorcerer.<br /><br />\\"Sherlock Holmes\\" isn't actually based on any Sherlock Holmes stories, but Guy Ritchie's sharp-edged direction and Downey's quirky performance make it a fun experience. B003Y5H5HO|4.0|The Swedish version of \\"The Girl With the Dragon Tattoo\\" is one of the greatest foreign films in recent years, mostly due to the brilliant Noomi Rapace.<br /><br />So an American remake sounds like an automatic fail, even one directed by David Fincher. But this remake is an unusually faithful one, especially for Hollywood -- not only is the film actually set in Sweden, but it preserves the bleak, icy atmosphere and violent undercurrents of the original. And while Rooney Mara is no Noomi, she and Daniel Craig do solid jobs as the leads.<br /><br />Take-no-prisoners journalist Mikael Blomkvist (Craig) has just lost his reputation, his savings and his freedom (hello, jail sentence!) after a nasty libel suit from a crooked executive.<br /><br />Then he's unexpectedly contacted by aged industrialist Henrik Vanger (Christopher Plummer), to discover what happened to the guy's grandniece, Harriet. She vanished years ago from the family's isolated island home, and her body -- alive or dead -- was never found. When Mikael agrees to take the case, he's cybershadowed by the abused goth hacker Lisbeth Salander (Mara).<br /><br />Eventually Mikael ends up finding out about Lisbeth, and her very unique skills. Intrigued by this strange wounded woman, Mikael asks her to help him in his cold-case investigation, and the two begin unwinding a bizarre string of serial killings from long ago -- all tied to Bible quotes. And as Mikael unearths the clues to Harriet's disappearance, he also finds some skeletons long kept buried -- and a killer who might target him next.<br /><br />\\"The Girl With the Dragon Tattoo\\" takes the typical murder mystery, and enfolds it in a ruthless look at modern Swedish society and the study of sexual aggression. It's a dark, dangerous, unfair world where the truth is quashed and women are treated horribly -- whether it's the long-missing Harriet or the eccentric, angry \\"girl with the dragon tattoo.\\"<br /><br />And Fincher gives it a surprisingly amount of loving care. No Americanized names, no switching the action to an American city, no soft-pedalling the gruesome sexual violence -- yep, that horrifying rape scene (and its gratifying payback) are here.<br /><br />Fincher isn't quite able to capture the wild, raw beauty of Sweden, but he is able to give the movie a somber, icy quality -- even in warmer months, it feels chilly and lonely. He unfolds the story with a minimum of Hollywood pyrotechnics, instead focusing on a collage of photographs, coded messages and old crimes half-forgotten. It's only in the last act that things start building into a bloody, creepy thriller.<br /><br />However, the wrap-up for the movie seems to drag. Yes, it's important to see what happens after the climax, especially if they're going to make a sequel -- but did we really need to SEE Lisbeth shopping for a wig?<br /><br />Rooney Mara doesn't quite fill the screen with dark dynamism as Lisbeth Salander should, but she does give a compelling performance -- a cold, almost cyborg-like presence that can suddenly explode into jagged icy rage. Daniel Craig's Mikael can't quite compete with Lisbeth, but Craig gives an outstanding performance as a weary journalist whose life is threatened by his encounter with the Vangers, and given new vitality by Lisbeth.<br /><br />\\"The Girl with the Dragon Tattoo\\" is a bleakly beautiful piece of work -- the actors are excellent and Fincher shows great respect to the original movie. Here's hoping for the tale of a girl playing with fire.ng care. B0095H4FAA|4.0|\\"The Girl With the Dragon Tattoo\\" sounds more like a kung-fu movie than a Swedish thriller.<br /><br />But a Swedish thriller it is. And while posthumous debuts rarely get the attention they deserve -- or any attention, really -- the first novel of the late Stieg Larsson's bestselling Millennium trilogy starts slow, and slowly winds itself up into a tight knot of unique mystery and thriller elements. Just give it a few chapters to unfold.<br /><br />Take-no-prisoners journalist Mikael Blomkvist has just lost his reputation, his savings and his freedom (hello, jail sentence!) after a nasty libel suit from an executive named Wennerstr&ouml;m. Then he's unexpectedly contacted by aged industrialist Henrik Vanger, to discover what happened to the guy's grandniece. She vanished forty years ago from the family's isolated island, and her body -- alive or dead -- was never found.<br /><br />It's a pretty cold case, and not a very inviting one. But when Vanger offers Mikael rewards he cannot refuse -- evidence on the villainous Wennerstr&ouml;m -- he finds himself facing the bizarre, sinister Vanger family with few leads to go on. Then he meets Lisbeth Salander, an eccentric, abused computer hacker who serves as his counterpoint. And as Mikael unearths the clues to Harriet's disappearance, he also finds some skeletons long kept buried.<br /><br />Posthumous debuts are a rarity -- publishers always want a series, sequels, publicity signings, and books headed by \\"By the Author Of...\\" You don't get any of that if the person is dead. So it's a sign of how amazing \\"The Girl With the Dragon Tattoo\\" is that Larsson's uniquely mystery-thriller has received such attention across the world.<br /><br />And Larsson's book is a unique blend of old and new -- he takes the Agatha Christie aesthetic of a locked-room murder with a dozen suspects, and enfolds it in a ruthless look at modern Swedish society and the study of sexual aggression. It's a dark, dangerous, unfair world where the truth is quashed and women are treated horribly -- whether it's the missing Harriet or the eccentric, angry \\"girl with the dragon tattoo.\\"<br /><br />And though his prose is rather bleak and often quite gritty, a certain brand of understated passion shines through Larsson's thriller story. It's the kind that feels the need to express itself even though it takes place in fiction. The only problem with the book is that Larsson pays undue attention to the whole Mikael-against-the-exec story. Not interesting, compared to the mystery.<br /><br />But Mikael and Salander make an intriguing odd couple -- he's so world-weary and demoralized that he seems to care about nothing, while Salander is a mass of hurts and quirks. While she sometimes seems cartoonish, she's also a vibrant little character with enough strength and power to lash back at attackers. The supporting characters are far darker fare, including a loopy Nazi weirdo.<br /><br />Take the works of Agatha Christie and smother them in a disillusioned, morally-bankrupt noir world -- and you'll have something like \\"The Girl With the Dragon Tattoo.\\" A hard read, but leaves you wondering what the next two books have in store.hed and women are treated horribly -- whether it's the missing Harriet or the eccentric, angry \\"girl with the dragon tattoo.\\"<br /><br />And though his prose is rather bleak and often quite gritty, a certain brand of understated passion shines through Larsson's thriller story. B0011UF79C|5.0|Imagine a world where your future is determined by your genes. Your schools, your job, your social status are all determined by what genetic flaws you have.<br /><br />Such is the world that we see in \\"Gattaca,\\" a scarily plausible sci-fi story that examines the implications of eugenics and genetic enhancement. Director Andrew Niccol thankfully never gets too preachy or \\"uplifting\\" -- instead he crafts a cleanly elegant story with a murder mystery as the catalyst, and the strong trinity Jude Law, Uma Thurman and Ethan Hawke.<br /><br />Vincent Freeman (Hawke) is an \\"invalid,\\" conceived without eugenic technology. On the day he was born, it was predicted that he would be myopic, might be bipolar, and would probably die at thirty from a heart defect. Throughout his life, Vincent dreams of becoming an astronaut, but his genetic status dooms him to menial labor.<br /><br />The solution: Vincent \\"buys\\" the identity and genetic profile of Jerome Eugene Morrow (Law), an Olympic swimmer who broke his back in a car crash. Vincent will pay Jerome, and Jerome will provide him with blood, urine, skin and hair samples.<br /><br />With Jerome's help, Vincent is accepted into Gattaca Aerospace Corporation, and is chosen for a manned flight to Titan. But when one of the administrators is found murdered, the police find one of Vincent's eyelashes nearby. If they discover who Vincent truly is, he won't just lose his dream -- he'll be convicted of murder.<br /><br />\\"Gattaca\\" is a movie that addresses one of life's great questions -- does destiny determine how we live our lives, or do we decide our own fate? There are no easy answers, but it's always best to err on the side of \\"we can make our own destiny\\" -- and this movie is basically all about one man's quest to determine his own fate, DNA be damned.<br /><br />None of the movies Andrew Niccol has made since \\"Gattaca\\" have even come close to this level of skill -- the movie is a slow, elegant winding of murder mystery and philosophical sci-fi. The plot quietly grows in tension as Vincent's life becomes more tangled up in lies and suspicion, with quietly powerful dialogue laced with dry humor (\\"I guess we can rule out suicide,\\" comments a cop as they bag a bloodied keyboard).<br /><br />And it's beautifully filmed. Everything is full of clean lines and cold light, with the occasional moment of symbolism -- note that the apartment that Eugene and Vincent share is centered around a giant DNA-style spiral staircase.<br /><br />And the final puzzle pieces are the actors, who are all really good. Ethan Hawke gives a quietly powerful performance as a man who goes to desperate lengths to fulfill his dreams, and Uma Thurman has the right mix of icy reserve and sweetness as a \\"valid\\" woman who falls in love with the man rather than his DNA.<br /><br />But the best performance is Jude Law. Jerome a truly tragic character, whose genetic perfection wasn't enough to keep disaster away -- he's proof that DNA can't make your life good or rewarding. Law plays him with a mixture of bitter wit and compassion, with perpetually shadowed eyes and a drink always in his hand. You may cheer for Vincent, but your heart will bleed for Jerome.<br /><br />\\"Gattaca\\" is a masterpiece of science fiction -- a richly nuanced, beautifully understated work that reminds you that, no matter where we come from, we can all reach for the stars.ical sci-fi. B00AQ5QB0W|5.0|Whenever you think of Jim Henson's Muppets, the fuzzy kooky variety shows and entertaining adventures of Miss Piggy, Gonzo and Kermit the Frog come to mind.<br /><br />But these versatile puppets have been used for far more dramatic effects in \\"The Dark Crystal,\\" an exquisite little fantasy story that happens to be entirely populated by puppets. Not a live person in sight, from start to finish. Henson and Brian Froud conjured up a gloriously detailed world -- full of fantastical flora and fauna, crystalline castles and strange creatures -- and wrapped it around a solid coming-of-age story.<br /><br />On another world, there are two strange races that appeared one thouysand years ago -- the enormous, gentle, peaceful Mystics, and the nasty, vulture-like, vicious Skekses who ruin the surrounding lands. Both races are somehow connected to a massive crystal that was broken a thousand years ago, and now a shard is missing from it. What's more, three suns are about to come into conjunction, and the shard has to be back in place when that happens.<br /><br />The Mystics have cared for one of the last Gelflings, an orphan named Jen whom they rescued from the insectile Garthim. As the conjunction approaches, they send him out to find the lost shard. Along the way, Jen gains the crystal shard for himself, and meets the only other living Gelfling, Kira. Now they must both evade the Skekses' Garthim henchmen and bat-winged spies, and somehow infiltrate their castle. But what will happen when the suns line up, and the crystal is completed?<br /><br />Many fantasy movies slap together a story that has been told many times before, but increasingly without interest -- throw in swords, some monsters, a brave prince or farm boy, and some evil witch/queen/wizard/Dark Lord. But fortunately nothing is so simple in \\"The Dark Crystal\\" -- although the origins of the Skekses and Mystics are pretty obvious from the very start of the movie, the journey along with Jen is what really makes this interesting.<br /><br />Instead of some pan-medieval land, \\"The Dark Crystal\\" aims at portraying a truly alien world, and Brian Froud's slightly weird designs make it seem so -- strange swamp reptiles, colorful anemones, vast crystalline castles, desiccated vulture-like creature, shrieking fuzzballs, and gorgeous forests full of strange plants and animals that tend to move and act in unique ways. \\"The Dark Crystal\\" leaves you feeling like you've been transported to some other planet.<br /><br />Additionally, this film also has the honor of being the first major movie to entirely star puppets, with nary a human being in sight -- some of those puppets still walk in a herky-jerky Muppet manner, but their design is utterly detailed and believable, from Jen's sort-of-human, fawnlike features to the vast four-armed Mystics, and the rattling armored Garthim soldiers. And the special effects end up being pretty spectacular, especially during the chaotic literally earthshattering climax -- while the finale is rather predictable, it is no less awe-inspiring for that.<br /><br />At times it feels like the characterization gets a backseat to the movie's visuals, but Jen and fellow Gelfling orphan Kira are fairly likable, naive young heroes who develop a cute little romance throughout the story. The various Skekses are given individual quirks and personalities (such as the annoying Chamberlain who won't stop humming). Not to mention the abrasive, lumpy witch Aughra, who's an absolute riot (\\"Whole world might burn up... hmph... end of Augra!\\") and Kira's toothy fuzzball Fizzgig.<br /><br />\\"The Dark Crystal\\" has some predictable plot twists, but it is also a glorious visual feast and a coming-of-age journey with the Gelflings, leading to a finale both awe-inspiring and bittersweet. Definitely a must-see.interesting.<br /><br />Instead of some pan-medieval land, \\"The Dark Crystal\\" aims at portraying a truly alien world, and Brian Froud's slightly weird designs make it seem so -- strange swamp reptiles, colorful anemones, vast crystalline castles, desiccated vulture-like creature, shrieking fuzzballs, and gorgeous forests full of strange plants and animals that tend to move and act in unique ways. \\"The Dark Crystal\\" leaves you feeling like you've been transported to some other planet.<br /><br />Additionally, this film also has the honor of being the first major movie to entirely star puppets, with nary a human being in sight -- some of those puppets still walk in a herky-jerky Muppet manner, but their design is utterly detailed and believable, from Jen's sort-of-human, fawnlike features to the vast four-armed Mystics, and the rattling armored Garthim soldiers. B005LAIIHG|5.0|I sat on the sofa with my husband and teen aged children. I knew Life of Pi had won awards but I needed to read reviews before deciding whether to watch it. I read the reviews on Amazon before watching this movie. In fact I usually come to Amazon before making a final decision on a film. The part that baffles me is the negative reviews of this film. I don't understand why anyone would have compared this to Avatar or why anyone after watching the entire film would bother bringing Avatar up. The only reason I can fathom as to why anyone would dislike this movie is if they didn't understand it. And that in itself baffles me too. Visually, this movie was stunning. Period. The cinematography and effects were gorgeous. Gorgeous not like Avatar...gorgeous like Life of Pi. The two are two very very different types of movie. But by the end of this movie I knew that it was not just a film for entertainment sake or about visual effects. It was human, raw, and frankly the story isn't about the tiger, it's about the boy who survived. The story is his. No matter what the real ending is, matters not. What matters is that we as humans face sometimes the most unfathomable trials and challenges and how we handle them is entirely up to us. When I finished watching this and I was done wiping my tears away, I knew I wanted to see it again. Somehow I felt like the lessons in the story weren't all going to be caught on the first try. It is worth watching again for the parts I may have missed. I was in awe of the cast and how effortlessly they played their parts. There were times when I laughed, times when I cried, times when I cringed and that is what a movie experience is supposed to be. No, that is what story telling is supposed to be. This film most definitely deserved academy awards. And I'll end this review...this very simplistic review with saying that I feel sorry for those that hated this movie. They missed out on the powerful message and the beautiful story. I can only be glad for myself that I got to experience Pi's story. I know I was transformed. .

1476733953|1.0|Wool is one of the worst books I have ever tried to read. I read about 100 pages of Wool--this thing--and then I thought, \\"You've got to be kidding. This is a joke, right?\\" I didn't have enough patience, or time, or will, to answer that question for myself or to determine whether the science made any sense. A tip of my hat goes to those with the strength of will to slog through this mess. This thing reads like a ninth-grade writing assignment (I know, because I still have my \\"story\\"). Wool reads no better than that, and it's several hundred times as long. The characters are cardboard cutouts, their actions are stock. The writing is awful, embarrassingly so. There's this deputy character near the beginning, Marnes, who's apparently of some rural or Southern extraction. The dialogue for this character is laughable, like this: \\"Truer than true, I reckon. Never figure to outlive so many. Sure as sin don't see me outliving you.\\" Give me a break. Never mind that people have been living in this damn silo for centuries (so I guess it's true what they say: You can take the hick out of the country, but you can't take the country out of the hick--It's genetic!). All the dialogue is of this caliber, although it's not all of the cracker sort. What I read felt like I ate 20 corn dogs one after the other. . . . Years ago I read Samuel Delany's Dhalgren, also a fat book (more than 800 pages). I read that book until about 10 pages from the end. Incomprehensible from the beginning, circling `round and `round. After days and days of reading and hoping to get some payoff, I couldn't go any further. I had to stop--had to--to retain any modicum of self-respect. I won't make that mistake again. Do not read Wool. You may come to hate yourself for the life you wasted.<br /><br />Wool Reflux<br /><br />First I read 100 pages of Wool, and I threw Wool against the proverbial wall. But then I had second thoughts. One part of me said I should give this book time to right itself, and another partof me got curious: How bad can it get? So, I picked the book off the floor, dusted it off, and, against my better judgment, gave it another go. I finished it, and I was right. It's one lousy read.<br /><br />Wool (and Mr. H) really ticked me off, and this is why. I hate investing what little spare time I have on a book, like Wool, that turns out to be obviously thrown together. If you're going to write a novel, you have to respect your readers. You're asking them for many hours of their time, and you owe them at least a half-hearted attempt at craft. Wool is such a heap of so many kinds of junk, it took my breath away. It fascinates me why people would like this thing. I could make a life's work out of cataloguing all the awfulness, categorizing all the classes and subclasses of crap, but what's the point of pissing away any more of my life? So here are just a few things--the curious, the dumbfounding, and the infuriating--about this book. First the curious. Most of the characters--male and female--seem like different masks of the same person. They have similar kinds of inner monologues, actions, and psychologies (takes on life, what gets them crying, angry, etc.). The characters act weirdly similar, as if they had emanated from a dream of someone who cannot imagine anyone unlike himself (i.e., a psychopath, or Tom Cruise). Is this the hidden key to Wool? Is it all a dream of a deranged mind? With no imagination? Alas, that's been done before (TV's Dallas), so maybe that is not Mr. H's intent. Now the dumbfounding. Stuff just doesn't add up, scientifically or otherwise. The dialogue is not only clunky, the way a character talks changes. Marnes first starts speaking with this ridiculous Southern or Western drawl. Then it disappears. I don't get it. I read these color-by-number exchanges, and the author can't even stay within the lines. And then we have the page after mind-numbing page of inner monologues and minutiae about some trivial thing a character is doing. Then I wised up and started skimming to get past these narrative ruts. It's like driving a washboard road. If you don't drive fast, it'll shake your car apart. That's how I had to read this book. I'm not saying describing something in minute detail never serves a purpose, but as someone else has noted, it's got to do that (read Cormac McCarthy's description of a character's fixing a shopping cart in The Road to see how it does work). Finally, the infuriating Mr. H does not create a plausible world. It's obvious he has not thought through how things would work in his silo world. Either that or he can't. OK, for instance, you've got this oil well down at the bottom of the silo somewhere. There's no description of this little detail, so I have to picture something on my own. What I come up with is an old pumpjack at the bottom of a shaft, just rocking away. This is absurd. I know it, so give me something I can latch onto! The silo is hermetically sealed off from the outside. The author needs to help the reader visualize how the silo works that way so the reader can suspend disbelief. Mr. H never does that. For example, how do you get rid of the gases that accompany oil extraction so you don't asphyxiate the entire population? And you've apparently got a smelter down deep to process ore from the mines (that burrow off in a single direction, apparently) into metal for homemade guns, nuts, bolts, pipe wrenches, etc. The smelter alone should do everyone in. It's never mentioned. The silo saga is set hundreds of years in the future, but the characters think and feel like what a high school kid from the suburbs of late 20th century New Jersey might imagine an adult of that era would think and feel like. I'm sorry, but you've got to consider how cultures might undergo change over hundreds of years (compare Earthlings in the 14th century with those in the 20th--not the same). This book is way beyond inconsistent; it's not remotely credible. If you like science fiction, there areso, so many places you can go and inhabit an interesting world with a consistent internal logic. Arthur Clarke's Rendezvous with Rama (the first Rama book), any Heinlein, Stanislaw Lem's Solaris and Fiasco, William Gibson's Neuromancer, Gibson and Sterling's The Difference Engine. And on and on. Go ahead, Google \\"best sci-fi books\\" and find something to your taste. You can do a hell of a lot better than Wool.<br /><br />P.S. I checked Wool out from the excellent public library in my neighborhood.

B000IOM1Z0|5.0|The Alan Parsons Project released its fifth album in as many years entitled The Turn of a Friendly Card in November of 1980.<br />After two OK albums out of 1978's Pyramid and 1979's Eve (both of which were pale in comparison to their 1976 debut Tales of Mystery and Imagination and its 1977 followup I Robot), The Alan Parsons Project were faced with losing its luster. In early 1980, Alan Parsons (well known engineer and producer who had worked with The Beatles as a tape operator and engineered Pink Floyd's Atom Heart Mother and classic Dark Side of the Moon and produced albums for Pilot, The Hollies and Al Stewart) and collaborator Eric Woolfson went to Monte Carlo as tax exiles to write a concept about the perils and dangers of gambling entitled The Turn of a Friendly Card and they (along with Alan Parsons Project musicians that were bass player David Paton and guitarist Ian Bairnson (formerly of the band Pilot known for its 1975 hit \\"Magic\\"), drummer Stuart Elliott (famed for his work on Al Stewart's Year of the Cat and Time Passages albums and on Paul McCartney's 1984 hit \\"No More Lonely Nights\\") and orchestrator Andrew Powell) went to France to record the album and a change of location and pace worked in the band's favor. Plus, ex-10cc members Kevin Godley and Lol Creme designed the album's sleeve instead of Hipgnosis (whom seemed to have an off year in 1980 laying low from sleeve designs apart from Peter Gabriel, The Scorpions and Mike Rutherford). When the album was released, it was at the time of rock and roll going through an identity crisis with metal, Hard rock, Corporate Rock and New Wave battling it out and also pop schlock was starting to come into fashion and would people find a prog rock concept album acceptable in 1980, well it did (as this and Pink Floyd's The Wall (which hit #1 earlier in 1980) proved).<br />We open the album with the uptempo rocker \\"May Be a Price to Pay\\". What a great opener and was the first album not to use an instrumental as an opening track and the lead vocals were sung great by Elmer Gentry. Next is the album's first single and a great song, the Top 20 charting \\"Games People Play\\" which was sung by Lenny Zatatek and its hypnotic keyboard keeps you on your toes the whole time and great guitar from Bairnson. Next is the album's second Top 20 hit which was the ballad \\"Time\\" which was sung by the late Eric Woolfson (which this song was his lead vocal debut on a Project track) and is a beautiful song about how time is to be taken for granted until it's gone and musically reminds one of \\"Us and Them\\" by Pink Floyd. We then segue into a nice piano and acoustic guitar doodle which goes into the first half's up tempo closer \\"I Don't Wanna Go Home\\", which was sung by Zatatek and is a great song about refusing to leave and the song ends with a reprise of the piano and acoustic guitar intro.<br />The second half of the album starts with the instrumental \\"The Gold Bug\\" which is a great instrumental featuring sax work by Mel Collins. Then we have the 16 and a half minute title suite which takes up the rest of the album. First is \\"The Turn of a Friendly Card Part 1\\" which is sung by Chris Rainbow and is a great song which sets the epic up for its peaks and valleys. The next part is \\"Snake Eyes\\" which is also sung by Rainbow and an awesome section. Next is the first instrumental section of this piece entitled \\"Ace of Swords\\" which is incredible. We then go into the country-ish sounding \\"You've Got Nothing Left to Lose\\" which has Woolfson on lead vocal and Chris Rainbow and Alan Parsons on harmonies and just a great song before going into an uncredited reprise of \\"Snake Eyes\\" featuring a great guitar solo by Ian Bairnson. \\"The Turn of a Friendly Card Part Two\\" closes things once again featuring vocals from Chris Rainbow and a great solo from Bairnson to close the album.<br />The Turn of a Friendly Card album became the Alan Parsons Project's biggest selling album since I Robot here in the US (and was their first to go Platinum since I Robot in 1977) and reached #13 in the US at the beginning of 1981.<br />The album was re-released in 2009 and was superbly remastered by Parsons himself with Dave Collins and features SIX bonus tracks including demos for \\"May Be a Price to Pay\\" and \\"The Gold Bug\\". Plus early run throughs of \\"You've Got Nothing Left to Lose\\". Then an early take of the ballad \\"Time\\" and a rough mix of \\"Games People Play\\" to close out the remastered album. These tracks are excellent different looks of each of the album's tracks.<br />Recommended!heir first to go Platinum since I Robot in 1977) and reached #13 in the US at the beginning of 1981.<br />The album was re-released in 2009 and was superbly remastered by Parsons himself with Dave Collins and features SIX bonus tracks including demos for \\"May Be a Price to Pay\\" and \\"The Gold Bug\\". These tracks are excellent different looks of each of the album's tracks.<br />Recommended!

B008B3MZNI|5.0|Supertramp's seventh album entitled Paris was released in October of 1980.<br />Now, a Blu-Ray (and DVD) has been released of the classic Supertramp performances from Paris in December of 1979 called Supertramp Live in Paris 1979.<br />The film was shot and recorded in December of 1979 in Paris, France at the Pavillion on the penultimate night of the band's Breakfast in America European tour. Supertramp Live in Paris is a superb concert film, capturing the band at the peak of their popularity when co-leader/guitarist/singer/keyboard player/songwriter Roger Hodgson was still with the group. When you consider that the possibility of Hodgson returning to Supertramp these days very doubtful, Paris becomes an extremely important live document of the classic lineup of the band, indeed. This concert film had been fabled for years and no footage had been officially released (aside \\"The Logical Song\\" and \\"Dreamer\\" as promo films) it wasn't until the master tapes of the December 1, 1979 performance were discovered in drummer Bob Siebenberg's barn in cow dung. Then longtime producer Peter Henderson and old concert sound engineer Russell Pope (whom produced the original album) painstakingly restored and transferred the audio from analog to digital and brilliantly mixed the audio. Then Peter Clifton (the same man who finished Led Zeppelin's The Song Remains the Same after Joe Massot bailed) salvaged the footage shot by Derek Burbidge (who filmed much of The Police's promo videos in the day) and added some bits to round out the film. What is available is a superb document of the classic Supertramp lineup.<br />Many great Supertramp classics are included in this live concert film, such as most of 1974's grand breakthrough Crime of the Century (the spirited opener \\"School\\", a slightly extended \\"Bloody Well Right\\", an excellent \\"Hide in Your Shell\\", a superb \\"Asylum', the Top 20 hit \\"Dreamer\\", a superb \\"Rudy\\" and the closing \\"Crime of the Century\\" which closes this live concert film), two songs from 1975's highly underrated followup Crisis? What Crisis? (\\"Another Man's Woman\\" and \\"Two of Us\\" both superior to studio versions), three from 1977's Gold selling US breakthrough Even in the Quietest Moments (the title cut, \\"Give a Little Bit\\" and the best version of \\"Fool's Overture\\" ever which has an ending that is to be seen to believe), and half of the #1 Multi-Platinum selling Breakfast in America (the monster hit \\"The Logical Song\\", the hit \\"Goodbye Stranger\\", the title cut, the Top 20 smash \\"Take The Long Way Home\\" and the epic closer \\"Child of Vision\\").<br />Hodgson and co-leader/keyboardist/singer/songwriter Rick Davies sing their hearts out and play their respective keyboards with great passion, and Hodgson also serves up some incredible fretwork on the guitar. Co-horts John Helliwell on saxophones/clarinets/occasional keyboards and additional backing vocals, drummer Bob C. Benberg and bass player Dougie Thomson have terrific interplay with Hodgson and Davies, helping to bring the band's great melodic music to life in tremendous fashion. Just like on their studio albums, the band totally cook live in concert, too. The songs on here rival their studio counterparts, especially on Rudy which is sped at faster speed than the studio version on the Roger/Rick vocal duel section and the visuals of the backdrop films are superb.<br />The songs that weren't shot for the film which were recorded (\\"Ain't Nobody But Me\\", \\"You Started Laughing\\", \\"Downstream\\", \\"A Soapbox Opera\\" and \\"From Now On\\") are here as bonus tracks with visuals created specifically for the DVD/Blu-Ray.<br />Also, much to Roger Hodgson's chagrin, the songwriting credits are printed on the back of the Blu-Ray (may not have been there on the first issue) and the Supertramp fans are just grateful that this classic document of the band's peak is FINALLY AVAILABLE!!!<br />Highly recommended!), two songs from 1975's highly underrated followup Crisis? PLUS, there are vital differences.<br /><br />The premise: just as the CERN atomic collider in Switzerland \\"fires up,\\" EVERYONE on earth falls asleep for two minutes.  It is during this worldwide \\"sleep\\" that they envision their own future roughly two decades from now.  When they awaken - and not all do - they find a world in severe trauma: during the last couple of unattended minutes a whole plethora of planes have crashed, cars, buses, boats and other vehicles have had accidents, etc, etc.<br /><br />Mind you, the essence of this premonition is not something they determine right away...it takes time for them to determine that their dreams all intertwine, and these dreams seem to be the actual future.  But it is at this point that these visions form the real problem...<br /><br />What is fate, and are our options in the face of it?<br /><br />What follows is an extremely well-depicted investigation of not only free will versus fate, but people's reactions to what they envisioned.  Imagine you saw yourself at your child's wedding, or happily sitting beside a person in a house: wouldn't you immediately set out to find out who those people were?  What if you were married, but saw yourself two decades later with another spouse?  What if your vision was of yourself in jail?  Or a millionaire?<br /><br />One scenario many encountered was related to watching a news report that covered America's president...but that boy was until this vision an average teenager.  Should America set about setting him up with a Secret Service detail?<br /><br />Sawyer examines such idiosyncrasies, and how they affect key characters within the scientific community.  For instance, one character learned of his own murder, and began trying to find the person who killed him...before it occurred.<br /><br />Or what if you dreamt nothing at all...what would that mean?<br /><br />Obviously this is a treasure trove for a good sci-fi writer, and in my opinion they don't get much better than Robert J. Sawyer.  He is not afraid of taking a phenomenon like this and trying to examine it both scientifically AND socially.  Sawyer excels at such tasks, and in one vital aspect therein: making it understandable and meaningful.  Though his books typically concern themselves with heady, intellectual matters, the way they play out is always handled with an eye toward rationality and plausibility.<br /><br />Are we able to escape our own \\"fates\\"? 0062020897|5.0|This is the book that \\"made\\" Follett, his first breakout bestseller, and there's no question why that happened once you start to read.<br /><br />In many ways, Eye of the Needle sets the pattern for his most successful efforts other than Pillars of the Earth: a deadly secret, a resourceful navigator of the shadow world of intrigue (sometimes the protagonist, sometimes the villain), at least one determined woman in the thick of it, and multi-party chases set against a ticking clock. All these devices work together seamlessly in this book. Follett even makes the saving-the-D-Day-secret plot thread seem fresh.<br /><br />Follett's WWII-based novels have always felt stronger to me than the others (again, with the exception of Pillars of the Earth); his settings in wartime England and occupied Europe are assured and immersive in a way some of his more modern settings are not. A few familiar tropes sneak in (the tweedy-professor-as-spycatcher, for instance, which Daniel Silva also used in The Unlikely Spy), although in 1978 they weren't as overused as they are now.<br /><br />If you haven't read Follett before, you should start with Eye of the Needle and his next bestsellers, Triple and The Key to Rebecca. If you only know him from Pillars of the Earth, your reaction to these books will depend on whether you enjoy spy thrillers as much as you do multi-generational Medieval epics. In any case, don't turn up your nose at these because they're thirty years old; they'll still show you a good time today.
